한국어

아프리카 유일 원전서 '오염' 사건 발생했지만 방사성 물질 외부 유출 없어, 규제기관 발표

원본: https://apnews.com/article/nuclear-reactor-south-africa-contamination-regulator-866adfe2fb7dab78878ae551843a5bd5

아프리카 유일 원전서 '오염' 사건 발생했지만 방사성 물질 외부 유출 없어, 규제기관 발표

요약

  • 카테고리: 현황
  • 남아프리카공화국 핵규제기관은 아프리카 유일의 원자력 발전소인 코버그 원전에서 최근 발생한 세 건의 '오염' 사건에서 방사성 물질이 환경으로 유출되지 않았다고 밝혔다.
  • 해당 사건들은 6월 30일, 7월 2일, 7월 7일 각각 발생했으며, 유지보수 작업 중 환기 장치 전원 손실로 인해 원전 내부에서 '공기 중 방사성 오염 수치 상승'이 발생했다.
  • 국가핵규제기관은 세 차례의 오염 사건 모두 발전소 내부에 국한되었으며 일반 대중에게는 위험이 없다고 밝혔다.

남아프리카공화국 케이프타운(Cape Town) 외곽에 위치한 코에베르크(Koeberg) 원자력 발전소, 2012년 2월 8일 수요일. (AP Photo/Schalk van Zuydam, File)

작성: 제럴드 임레이(GERALD IMRAY)

업데이트 [시]:[분] [오전/오후] [시간대], [월] [일], [년]

스페인어로 읽기

구글에서 AP 뉴스 추가 더 많은 기사를 보려면 구글에서 AP 뉴스를 기본 소스로 설정하세요. 공유

케이프타운, 남아프리카공화국 (AP) — 남아프리카공화국의 원자력 규제 기관은 목요일, 아프리카 유일의 원자력 발전소에서 최근 발생한 세 차례의 "오염" 사건 동안 방사성 물질이 환경으로 유출되지 않았다고 밝혔다.

국가원자력규제위원회(National Nuclear Regulator)에 따르면, 이번 사건들은 유지보수 작업 중 환기 장치에 전력 공급이 차단되면서 남아프리카공화국 서해안에 위치한 코에베르크(Koeberg) 발전소 내부에서 "공기 중 방사성 오염 수준이 상승"한 것과 관련이 있으며, 공공 안전에는 위험이 없다고 덧붙였다.

규제 기관은 6월 30일, 7월 2일, 7월 7일에 발생한 세 차례의 개별 오염 사건이 모두 발전소 내부에 국한되었다고 밝혔다.

발전소 내부에서 방사선에 노출되었을 가능성이 있는 작업자들에 대해 선별 검사가 실시되었으며, 기록된 방사성 오염 수치는 치과 X선 촬영 시 사람이 노출되는 방사선량보다 낮은 수준이었다고 원자력규제위원회(NNR)는 밝혔다.

규제 기관은 추가 검사를 진행하는 한편, 최근의 사건들이 "원자력 또는 방사선 사건이나 비상사태로 분류되는 기준을 충족하지 않았으며, 부지 외부로의 방사선 영향도 발생하지 않았다"고 발표했다.

코버그(Koeberg) 발전소는 남아프리카 제2의 도시 케이프타운 북쪽 약 40킬로미터(25마일) 지점에 위치해 있다. 이 발전소는 아프리카 유일의 상업용 원자력 발전소로, 아파르트헤이트 시절인 1980년대에 가동을 시작했다. 두 기의 원자로를 보유하고 있으며 남아프리카 전력의 약 5%를 생산하고, 국영 전력회사인 에스콤(Eskom)이 운영하고 있다.

두 원자로는 최근 20년 운영 연장을 승인받아 2040년 이후까지 가동이 가능해졌다.

관련 기사

알제리 고아원 화재로 어린이 11명 사망, 19명 부상

1분 읽기

수십 년 만에 아프리카 최초의 백인 국가원수였던 잠비아의 가이 스콧(Guy Scott), 82세로 별세

1분 읽기

아마존, 남아프리카에 위성 인터넷 서비스 출시 — 머스크의 고향에서 스타링크보다 앞서는 듯

2분 읽기

이란 전쟁으로 인한 에너지 충격은 일부 국가들의 원자력 발전을 촉진하고 있으며, 아프리카와 아시아의 피해가 큰 국가들의 원자력 욕구에 더욱 힘을 실어주고 있다.

남아프리카는 불안정하고 오염을 유발하는 에너지 공급을 뒷받침하기 위해 신규 발전소를 건설하여 상업용 원자력 역량을 확대할 계획을 갖고 있으며, 현재 에너지 공급은 증가하는 인구를 감당하기 어려운 상황으로 석탄 연소에 크게 의존하고 있다.

더 읽기

다른 국가들도 원자력 사고를 반대하는 측에서 제기하는 체르노빌(Chernobyl)과 최근의 일본 후쿠시마(Fukushima) 같은 재난에 대한 오랜 안전 우려에도 불구하고, 급증하는 에너지 수요를 충족하기 위해 원자력 에너지로 더욱 눈을 돌리고 있다.

아프리카의 여러 다른 국가들도 자체적인 상업용 원자력 계획을 추진하고 있으며, 이집트의 경우 세계원자력협회(World Nuclear Association)에 따르면 러시아제 대형 원자로 4기를 갖춘 첫 번째 원자력 발전소를 건설 중으로, 2030년경 가동을 시작해 국가 전력의 약 10%를 생산할 것으로 기대하고 있다.

___

AP 아프리카 뉴스: https://apnews.com/hub/africa

AI 분석

남아프리카공화국의 국가핵규제청(NNR)은 아프리카 유일의 원자력 발전소인 코에버그(Koeberg) 원자력 발전소에서 발생한 세 차례의 '오염' 사건(6월 30일, 7월 2일, 7월 7일)과 관련하여 방사성 물질이 외부 환경으로 유출되지 않았다고 확인했다. 이번 사건들은 유지보수 작업 중 환기 장치의 전원 공급이 중단되면서 발전소 내부에서 공기 중 방사성 오염이 일시적으로 상승한 것으로, 모두 시설 내부에 격리·통제되었다. 이 사건은 핵시설에서의 유지보수 작업 중 안전 관리의 중요성을 다시 한번 부각시키며, 특히 케이프타운 인근에 위치한 코에버그 발전소의 운영 안전성에 대한 공공의 관심을 촉발시킬 수 있다. 남아프리카는 아프리카 대륙에서 유일하게 상업용 원자력 발전소를 운영하고 있는 국가로, 이번 사건은 향후 원전 운영 절차 및 비상 대응 체계 개선에 중요한 교훈을 제공한다. 규제 당국의 신속하고 투명한 정보 공개는 공공 신뢰 유지에 기여했지만, 동일한 유형의 사건이 단기간에 세 차례 발생했다는 점은 시스템적 취약성에 대한 면밀한 검토 필요성을 시사한다.

※ 이 글은 해외 원자력 바로알기를 위한 정보 전달을 목적으로 제공됩니다. 특정 기업이나 종목에 대한 투자 권유가 아니며, 모든 투자 판단과 그에 따른 책임은 투자자 본인에게 있습니다.

한국어

차세대 원자력 발전소 허가 및 운영에서의 인적 요소

원본: https://www.ans.org/news/article-8161/the-human-factor-in-licensing-and-operating-the-next-generation-of-nuclear-plants/

차세대 원자력 발전소 허가 및 운영에서의 인적 요소

2026년 7월 16일

요약

  • 카테고리: 기술
  • 소형 모듈 원자로(SMR), 마이크로 원자로 등 첨단 설계의 등장으로 원자력 산업이 근본적 전환기를 맞이하고 있으며, 디지털 계측제어 및 자동화 확대로 운전원의 역할이 수동 조작에서 감시·진단·예외 관리 중심으로 변화하고 있다.
  • 원격·다중 유닛·자율 운전 개념이 등장하면서 물리적 근접성 없이 이상 징후를 감지할 수 있는 명확한 디지털 인터페이스와 견고한 통신 시스템의 중요성이 부각되고 있다.
  • 높은 수준의 자율 시스템이 도입될수록 운전원이 자동화된 동작을 이해하고 개입 시점을 판단해야 하는 인적 요소 과제가 새로운 방식으로 복잡해지고 있다.
Industry

차세대 원자력 발전소 인허가 및 운영에서의 인적 요소

2026년 7월 16일, 오후 9시 46분 Nuclear News 스테파니 모로우(Stephanie Morrow)와 니아브 휴즈 그린(Niav Hughes Green)

원자력 인적 요소 연구에 활용되는 원자로 시스템 지표 및 경보를 표시하는 다중 패널 터치스크린 디스플레이. (사진: NRC)

인적 성능과 원자력 운영의 접점에서 근무하는 인적 요소 전문가로서, 우리는 수십 년 만에 가장 중요한 원자력 분야의 전환을 목격하고 있습니다. 소형 모듈 원자로, 마이크로 원자로 및 기타 첨단 설계의 등장은 산업 지형을 재편하고 있습니다. 디지털 계측 및 제어, 수동적 안전 시스템, 그리고 자동화 확대는 더 높은 안전 여유도와 더 유연한 운영을 위한 기회를 만들어내고 있습니다. 이와 같은 특성들은 원자력 발전소를 "운영"한다는 것의 의미를 근본적으로 재정의하기도 합니다. 인적 역할, 자동화, 수동 시스템 간의 상호작용은 사람들이 상황 인식을 유지하고, 판단을 내리며, 필요 시 개입하는 방식을 결정합니다. 이러한 발전은 운영 현실과 원자력 안전이 구축되는 규제 기반 모두에 영향을 미칩니다.

변화하는 역할

이 변화는 운전원의 역할에서부터 시작됩니다. 수십 년 동안 운전원들은 아날로그 제어 장치를 조작하고, 현장 지시계를 해석하며, 진동이나 소리 같은 감각적 신호에 의존하면서 전문성을 쌓아왔습니다. 첨단 원자로에서 운전원들은 대신 디지털 인터페이스를 통해 고도로 자동화된 시스템을 감독하게 됩니다. 일상적인 운영 중에는 수동 조작을 거의 수행하지 않으며, 그 역할은 모니터링, 진단, 예외 사항 관리 쪽으로 이동합니다.

이러한 변화는 디지털 정보의 품질에 더욱 큰 비중을 두게 합니다. 비정상 상태를 감지하는 데 도움이 되는 물리적 신호가 부족한 상황에서, 운전원들은 디스플레이가 발전소 상태를 얼마나 명확하게 구성하고 전달하는지에 전적으로 의존하게 됩니다. 운전원들은 장비에 물리적으로 근접하는 대신 인터페이스 설계, 데이터 통합, 투명한 자동화 동작을 통해 상황 인식을 유지합니다. 이러한 변화는 수동 제어가 아닌 감독 제어에 맞게 조직이 운전원을 양성하고, 주제어실을 설계하며, 절차와 훈련을 개발하는 방식을 재고하도록 요구합니다.

새로운 운영 개념

NRC 인적 성능 시험 시설에 있는 INL이 개발한 랜코 마이크로월드(Rancor Microworld) 시뮬레이터의 데스크톱 워크스테이션 디스플레이. 디스플레이에는 여러 원자로 유닛과 인적 요소 연구에 활용되는 디지털 절차가 포함된 단순화된 제어 인터페이스가 표시되어 있습니다. (사진: NRC)

우리는 원격, 다중 유닛, 자율 운영 등 새롭게 등장하는 첨단 원자로 설계 개념과 그것이 인적 성능에 미치는 영향을 면밀히 추적하고 있습니다. 이러한 접근 방식 중 일부는 단일 부지 내 여러 원자로 모듈 또는 지리적으로 분산된 여러 원자로 유닛을 중앙 집중식 제어 센터에서 관리하도록 합니다. 일부 개념에서는 운영 책임이 완전히 부지 밖에 있으며, 강력한 디지털 통신 시스템으로 지원됩니다.

이러한 개념들은 효율성 향상의 가능성을 제공하는 동시에, 뚜렷한 인적 성능 과제도 수반합니다. 물리적 근접성 없이는 인터페이스가 이상 징후 감지를 지원하기 위해 명확하고 실행 가능한 정보를 전달해야 합니다. 통신 시스템은 정상 운영뿐만 아니라 비정상 사건, 사이버 장애, 연결성이 저하된 기간도 지원할 수 있을 만큼 강력해야 합니다. 분산된 팀은 명확하게 정의된 역할과 권한이 필요하며, 책임 분담이 불명확하면 위기 상황에서 망설임이나 상충되는 행동으로 이어질 수 있습니다.

자율 운영 개념은 또 다른 복잡성을 더합니다. 시스템이 더욱 적응적이거나 독립적인 행동을 취하게 됨에 따라, 근본적인 질문은 _자율 시스템은 얼마나 자율적인가?_ 가 됩니다. 높은 수준의 자율성에 더하여, 예상치 못한 상황에서 운전원이 여전히 개입해야 할 수 있다는 기대는 운전원의 제어가 기본적인 수동 정지 기능에만 한정된 설계와는 현저히 다른 인적 요소 과제를 만들어냅니다. 운전원은 자동화된 조치를 이해하고, 한계를 예측하며, 시스템 동작을 효과적으로 감독해야 합니다.

새로운 맥락 속의 오랜 과제들

기술과 운영 환경이 변화하고 있음에도 불구하고, 고전적인 인적 요소 과제들은 여전히 남아 있으며 오히려 더욱 두드러지는 경우가 많습니다.

상황 인식은 인간이 드물게 개입하다가 역동적이거나 비정상적인 사건 중에 갑자기 재참여할 때 저하될 수 있다. 자동화는 일상적인 작업 부하를 줄일 수 있지만, 비정상적인 조건에서 낮은 참여 기간 이후 갑작스러운 인지적 수요 급증이 뒤따르는 패턴을 초래할 수도 있다. 이 패턴은 현재 운영에서도 관찰되지만 첨단 원자로에서는 더욱 두드러질 것으로 예상된다. 자동화에 대한 신뢰는 적절하게 조율되어야 하며, 과신과 불신 모두 오류나 불필요한 개입으로 이어질 수 있다.

디지털 환경은 모드 혼동, 잘못된 단위 조작, 또는 정보가 밀집된 디스플레이에서의 신호 누락과 같은 새로운 오류 경로를 도입한다. 운영자가 중요한 수동 작업을 드물게 수행할 때 기술 유지 또한 과제가 된다. 드물지만 높은 결과를 초래하는 사건에 운영자가 계속 대비할 수 있도록 보장하기 위해 정기적인 훈련, 현실적인 시뮬레이션, 시나리오 기반 실습이 필수적이다.

마지막으로, 첨단 원자로는 신규 진입자나 여러 부지에 걸쳐 운영단을 관리하는 조직이 운영할 수 있으므로 조직적 요인이 점점 더 중요해지고 있다. 훈련 프로그램은 운영 조직보다는 개발자나 공급업체가 주도하여 중앙 집중화될 수 있다. 인원 절감, 분산 운영, 공급업체와 면허 보유자 간의 공동 책임은 기관 지식, 조정 및 문화에 부담을 줄 수 있다. 원자력 안전 문화는 공개적인 소통, 우려 사항 제기 독려, 원자력 안전을 최우선으로 하는 의사 결정 보장 등을 포함하여 의도적으로 육성되어야 한다.

여러 산업 분야가 자동화, 원격 운영, 분산 팀 구조와 관련된 유사한 과제에 직면해 왔다. 항공 분야는 조종사-자동화 상호 작용 관리, 모드 혼동 해결, 감독 역할에서의 경계심 유지에 관한 광범위한 증거를 제공한다. 해양 및 해상 에너지 운영은 원격 모니터링 센터와 분산 팀 조정에 관한 경험을 강조한다. 우주 시스템은 높은 수준의 자동화 관리와 드물지만 높은 결과를 초래하는 개입에 대한 운영자 준비 보장에 관한 교훈을 제공한다. 이러한 분야 전반에 걸쳐 공통 주제가 나타난다: 자동화 동작 검증, 시스템 의도를 드러내는 인터페이스 설계, 명확한 권한 구조 및 소통 경로 유지. 이러한 교훈 중 상당수는 첨단 원자력 분야에 매우 적용 가능하며 현재의 연구 및 지침 개발에 반영되고 있다.

현대 기술을 위한 규제 프레임워크

메릴랜드주 록빌(Rockville, Md.)에 위치한 NRC 본사. (사진: NRC)

규제는 기술과 함께 진화하고 있다. 2026년, 원자력규제위원회(Nuclear Regulatory Commission, NRC)는 첨단 상업용 원자로의 면허를 지원하도록 설계된 위험 기반, 기술 포괄적 규칙인 10 CFR Part 53을 최종 확정했다. 마이크로원자로 및 기타 저위험 설계를 위한 동반 프레임워크인 10 CFR Part 57은 개발 중이다.

이러한 규칙 제정은 1979년 스리마일 아일랜드(Three Mile Island) 사고 이후 도입된 오래된 원칙들을 기반으로 하며, 인간 요인 공학(Human Factors Engineering, HFE)의 중요성을 재확인한다. Part 53은 이를 명시적으로 밝히고 있다: 10 CFR 53.730(a)는 "플랜트 설계는 플랜트 안전 또는 비상 대응 기능의 지속적인 가용성을 수행하거나 지원하기 위해 인간 활동이 예상되는 모든 장소에서 안전하고 신뢰할 수 있는 성능을 위한 최첨단 인간 요인 원칙을 반영해야 한다"고 요구한다.

Part 53의 핵심 지침 문서 중 하나인 _확장 가능한 인간 요인 공학 검토 계획 임시 직원 지침 개발_(DRO-ISG-2023-03)은 확장 가능한 HFE 검토 프로세스를 도입한다. 이 접근 방식은 NRC 직원이 각 설계의 안전 중요도, 참신성, 인간 성능 영향에 맞게 검토 깊이를 조정할 수 있도록 한다. 모든 원자로에 균일한 수준의 검토를 적용하는 대신, 이 등급별 접근 방식은 고도로 자동화된 설계, 원격 운영 또는 새로운 인력 배치 모델과 같이 안전에 대한 인간의 기여가 가장 중요한 영역에 초점을 맞춘다. 이는 신청자에게 더 명확한 기대치를 제공하고 효율적이고 예측 가능한 면허 결과를 지원한다.

그러나 HFE 면허 검토에 대한 이러한 등급별 접근 방식이 전체 설계 수명 주기에 걸쳐 HFE 원칙을 통합하는 것의 중요성을 감소시키지는 않는다. 사실, 인간 중심적 고려 사항이 새로운 운영 개념에 어떻게 녹아 있는지를 보여줌으로써 신청자는 규제 신뢰를 구축하고 혁신과 안전이 상호 강화됨을 입증할 수 있다.

면허에 HFE 원칙 적용

혁신적인 설계에서 가장 중요한 단계 중 하나는 운영 개념을 조기에 정의하는 것입니다. 이는 인적 요소 공학(HFE) 프로그램과 규제 검토 모두의 기반이 됩니다. 예를 들어, 파트 53에 따라 설계를 자립형 완화 시설(self-reliant mitigation facility)로 분류할지 상호작용 의존형 완화 시설(interaction-dependent mitigation facility)로 분류할지는 HFE 활동의 범위와 깊이를 결정하는 핵심 사안입니다. "자립형 완화 시설"과 "상호작용 의존형 완화 시설"은 10 CFR 53.725(c)에서 원자력 발전소 설계가 10 CFR 53.800에 명시된 운영 및 기술 기준을 충족하는지 여부에 따라 정의됩니다. 이 시설 유형을 지칭하는 용어는 운전원이 사건을 완화하고 안전한 결과를 달성하기 위해 발전소 시스템과 상호작용할 것으로 예상되는 방식의 차이를 반영합니다. 이러한 시스템은 운전원이 어떤 방식으로든 상호작용해야 하거나(상호작용 의존형), 또는 운전원의 개입 없이 자체 능력에 완전히 의존할 수 있습니다(자립형).

명확한 운영 개념은 발전소가 어떻게 기능할지뿐만 아니라 설계가 인간의 필요, 능력, 한계를 어떻게 지원하는지도 설명해야 합니다. 이러한 결정을 인간 중심적 관점에서 구성하면 미래의 운영이 직관적이고 안전하며 실제 사람들이 일하는 방식에 부합하도록 보장하는 데 도움이 됩니다.

테네시주 채터누가에 있는 기술 훈련 센터(Technical Training Center)의 미국 원자력규제위원회(NRC) 글래스 패널 시뮬레이터(Glass Panel Simulator)에는 터치스크린 콘솔과 디지털 디스플레이 패널이 실제 크기의 제어실 레이아웃으로 배치되어 있습니다. (사진: NRC)

기술과 운영 맥락이 변하더라도 핵심 HFE 설계 원칙은 여전히 적용됩니다. 기능 요건 분석 및 기능 배분은 발전소의 필수 안전·운영 기능을 식별한 다음, 각 기능을 인간, 자동화, 수동적 특성, 고유한 설계 특성, 또는 이들의 조합 중 어느 것이 수행해야 하는지를 결정하는 과정을 포함합니다. 현대적인 기능 배분 방법은 인간과 자동화가 안전 및 성능 목표를 달성하기 위해 어떻게 협력하는지를 강조합니다.

작업 분석은 이를 기반으로 운전원이 자신의 책임을 효과적으로 수행하기 위해 무엇을 인지하고, 결정하고, 수행해야 하는지를 파악합니다. 첨단 원자로의 경우, 이는 여러 호기에 걸쳐 상황 인식을 유지하는 데 필요한 정보를 식별하고, 자동화된 동작을 이해하고, 비정상 사건 중 시스템 상태를 진단하고, 드물지만 중요한 수동 작업을 수행하는 것을 포함할 수 있습니다. 작업 분석 결과물은 인터페이스 설계, 절차, 인력 배치, 훈련에 반영됩니다.

인간-시스템 인터페이스는 인간-시스템 통합의 가장 가시적인 구성 요소 중 하나입니다. 디지털 환경은 뛰어난 유연성을 제공하지만 비일관성, 정보 과부하, 투명성 저하의 가능성을 초래할 수 있습니다. 디스플레이가 운전원의 정신 모델과 일치하고, 통합적이고 직관적인 방식으로 정보를 제시하며, 자동화 시스템의 의도를 명확하게 드러낼 때 운전원의 성과가 가장 우수합니다.

검증 및 확인은 HFE의 핵심입니다. 검증 및 확인은 실제 운영 조건을 반영한 성과 기반 평가를 통해 설계 가정을 시험합니다. 통합 시스템 확인을 통해 신청자는 운전원이 정상 및 비정상 시나리오 모두에서 효과적으로 작업을 수행할 수 있음을 입증하여 전반적인 설계가 안전 운영을 지원할 수 있다는 증거를 제공합니다. 이는 현실적인 시나리오, 광범위한 표본 추출 전략, 그리고 실제 조건 하에서 작업 부하, 작업 수행, 중요 행동이 허용 가능한 한계 내에 유지되는지 확인하기 위한 운전원 행동의 면밀한 관찰에 의존합니다.

연구 우선순위

랜코어 마이크로월드(Rancor Microworld) 시뮬레이터를 사용한 원자로 및 냉각재 시스템 지시계 클로즈업. (사진: NRC)

첨단 원자로 배치의 미래를 바라보며, NRC의 인적 요인 연구 상당 부분은 첨단 원자로 허가 심사에 대한 규제 준비에 초점을 맞추고 있습니다. 저희 연구는 이러한 기술을 효과적으로 평가하는 데 필요한 기술적 근거와 지침을 구축하는 것을 목표로 합니다.

우선 연구 분야 중 하나는 원격 운영의 인적 요인적 함의를 이해하는 것입니다. 원격 운영에서는 중앙집중식 제어 시설에서 운영을 수행하거나, 현장 인력과 원격지 인력 간에 책임을 분담하는 혼합 방식을 통해 운영이 이루어질 수 있습니다. _첨단 원자로를 위한 원격 운영: 인적 요인 공학 기술 기반 및 지침 권고사항_(RIL 2026-01)은 이러한 새로운 개념을 검토하고 향후 규제 지침을 지원하기 위한 핵심 인적 요인 과제를 식별합니다.

저희는 또한 디지털 제어 환경이 운전원의 작업 부하, 인체공학, 사용성에 미치는 영향을 연구하고 있습니다. _인간 성능 시험 시설 7권: 하드 패널과 글래스 탑 시뮬레이터 인체공학, 작업 부하 및 사용성의 차이_(RIL 2022-11)에서 얻은 통찰은 디지털 시스템이 성능을 향상시키는 부분과 설계를 통해 완화해야 하는 새로운 취약점을 초래할 수 있는 부분을 이해하는 데 도움이 됩니다.

또 다른 중요한 진전은 NUREG-0700(4판), _인간-시스템 인터페이스 설계 검토 지침_의 발행입니다. 업데이트된 지침에는 디지털 제어실의 디스플레이, 경보, 탐색 구조 및 기타 인터페이스 요소를 평가하기 위한 기준이 포함되어 있습니다. 이러한 지침을 최신 기술과 일치시키면 일관되고 예측 가능한 허가 심사를 지원합니다.

또한 원전 시스템의 자율성 증가에 대비하고 있습니다. _첨단 원자로 기술에서 자율성의 예상 활용에 대한 인적 요인 고려사항_(INL/RPT-25-89289)은 첨단 원자로 설계자들이 운영에 더 높은 수준의 자율성을 어떻게 적용할 수 있는지에 대한 현황을 탐구합니다. _첨단 원자로의 자율 운영: 인적 요인 공학 주제 특성화 및 필요성 평가_(RIL 2026-02)에서는 "자율성" 및 "자율 운영"과 같은 개념을 정의하고, 더 높은 수준의 자율성에 따른 인적 요인 고려사항과 그것이 NRC 심사 지침의 필요성에 미치는 영향을 탐구합니다. 이를 보완하여, 학술지 _Energies_에 발표된 논문 "기능 배분의 역사는 새로운 원자력 발전소에서 자동화의 역할에 대해 무엇을 말해줄 수 있는가"(doi.org/10.3390/en19010220)는 현대 자동화가 전통적인 인간 대 기계 모델보다 더 세밀한 배분 전략을 필요로 하는 이유를 강조합니다.

앞으로 우리의 지속적인 연구 노력에는 기능 배분, 원격 및 다중 설비 운영, 중요한 인간 행동의 변경을 수반하는 심사에 대한 지침 업데이트에 관한 지속적인 작업이 포함됩니다. 또한 자율 시스템이 인간의 역할을 어떻게 재구성하는지 평가하고 규제기관이 심사가 발전하는 기술에 맞게 조정되도록 하는 데 필요한 지침을 개발함으로써 자율 운영에 관한 연구를 발전시키고 있습니다.

사람, 기술, 조직의 조화

테네시주 채터누가에 위치한 NRC 기술훈련센터. (사진: NRC)

우리의 연구를 통해 하나의 통합적인 주제가 명확해집니다: 인간의 역할은 사라지는 것이 아니라 진화하고 있다는 것입니다. 첨단 원자로는 놀라운 잠재력을 제공하지만, 처음부터 인간의 성과를 지원하는 방식으로 설계될 때만 가능합니다. 설계, 훈련, 인력 배치, 조직 구조 전반에 걸쳐 인적 요인을 통합하는 것이 이를 달성하는 데 핵심적입니다. 궁극적으로, 첨단 원자력의 약속은 안전하고 신뢰할 수 있으며 회복력 있는 시스템을 만들기 위해 사람, 기술, 조직을 조화시키는 데 달려 있습니다.

이 분야의 전문가로서 우리는 연구를 발전시키고 규제 명확성을 지원하는 데 계속 헌신합니다. 신중한 계획과 열린 대화를 통해, 우리는 차세대 원자력 발전소가 산업계와 대중이 기대하는 높은 안전 및 성과 기준을 달성할 수 있다고 믿습니다.

_스테파니 모로우(Stephanie Morrow)와 니아브 휴즈 그린(Niav Hughes Green)은 NRC 원자력 규제 연구실의 인적 요인 심리학자입니다._

AI 분석

이 기사는 차세대 원자력 발전소(소형 모듈 원자로, 마이크로 원자로 등)의 등장으로 인해 운전원의 역할이 근본적으로 변화하고 있음을 심층적으로 분석하고 있다. 디지털 계측·제어 시스템과 고도의 자동화로 인해 운전원은 기존의 아날로그 장치 조작 중심에서 시스템 감시·진단·예외 관리 역할로 전환되고 있으며, 이는 전혀 새로운 교육 패러다임과 제어실 설계를 요구한다. 특히 원격 운전, 다중 호기 제어, 완전 자율 운전 개념이 부상하면서 물리적 근접성 없이 상황 인식을 유지하고 이상 상황에 신속히 개입하는 인간 능력의 한계와 인터페이스 설계의 중요성이 더욱 부각된다. 자동화 수준이 높아질수록 평상시 인지 부하는 줄어들지만, 비정상 상황 발생 시 갑작스러운 인지 과부하라는 오랜 인간 공학적 과제가 새로운 환경에서 더욱 심화될 수 있다는 점도 중요한 시사점이다. 이 기사는 규제 체계와 안전 기준이 이러한 기술적·운영적 변화를 선제적으로 반영해야 한다는 점을 강조하며, 원자력 산업의 안전하고 효율적인 미래를 위한 인간 요소 공학의 전략적 중요성을 잘 보여준다.

※ 이 글은 해외 원자력 바로알기를 위한 정보 전달을 목적으로 제공됩니다. 특정 기업이나 종목에 대한 투자 권유가 아니며, 모든 투자 판단과 그에 따른 책임은 투자자 본인에게 있습니다.

Aalo Atomics selects-PR Newswire

Source: https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/rockwell-automation-selected-by-aalo-atomics-to-power-control-systems-for-aalo-x-critical-test-reactor-302827652.html

Rockwell Automation Selected by Aalo Atomics to Power Control Systems for Aalo-X Critical Test Reactor

Published: July 16, 2026

TL;DR

  • Rockwell Automation's ControlLogix® platform selected as the control system provider for Aalo Atomics' Aalo-X test reactor
  • Aalo achieved criticality on its pilot reactor ahead of its July 4, 2026 deadline, marking a major milestone in next-generation nuclear deployment
  • Collaboration supports Aalo's participation in the U.S. Department of Energy Reactor Pilot Program to accelerate advanced nuclear technology development

Full Article

MILWAUKEE, July 16, 2026 — Rockwell Automation, Inc. (NYSE:ROK), the world's largest company dedicated to industrial automation and digital transformation, today announced Aalo Atomics, the company building fully modular nuclear plants to power modern AI data centers, has selected Rockwell as the control platform provider for its Aalo-X test reactor.

Collaboration Details

The collaboration supports Aalo's participation in the U.S. Department of Energy Reactor Pilot Program, an initiative to accelerate the development, authorization and validation of advanced nuclear technologies. Aalo reached criticality on its pilot reactor two weeks ago, ahead of its July 4, 2026, deadline, marking a significant milestone for next-generation nuclear deployment in the United States.

Rockwell Automation provides integrated control and information solutions, including its ControlLogix® platform, to support reactor operations, system reliability and accelerated development timelines. The platform is designed to deliver safe, scalable control for a first-of-its-kind reactor system across the full lifecycle, from design to operation.

Industry Perspective

"This collaboration highlights the growing need for proven industrial control systems to enable new energy technologies at scale," said Brian Holte, VP, Global Industry Sales at Rockwell Automation. "By supporting Aalo's path to first criticality, we're demonstrating how flexible, resilient platforms can accelerate the commercialization of advanced reactor designs."

Aalo's Aalo-X test reactor serves as a testbed for rapid innovation in modular reactor technology, allowing real-world validation of system performance and operational readiness. Through the DOE pilot program, Aalo has demonstrated a streamlined pathway to advance next-generation nuclear capabilities in a live environment.

"Rockwell brings deep expertise in mission-critical control systems that are essential for achieving our accelerated program milestones," said Yasir Arafat, President & CTO, Aalo Atomics. "Having a trusted automation partner is key to executing safely and efficiently and will help us pave the way towards commercial power."

The project positions Rockwell as a key enabler of emerging nuclear technologies and reinforces its role in supporting energy transition efforts through advanced automation and digital solutions. The companies will continue to collaborate as the program advances, with the Aalo-X test reactor serving as a foundation for future commercial deployments.

About Rockwell Automation

Rockwell Automation, Inc. (NYSE: ROK), is a global leader in industrial automation and digital transformation. We connect the imaginations of people with the potential of technology to expand what is humanly possible, making the world more productive and more sustainable. Headquartered in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, Rockwell Automation employs approximately 26,000 problem solvers dedicated to our customers in more than 100 countries as of fiscal year end 2025. To learn more about how we are bringing Connected Enterprise to life across industrial enterprises, visit www.rockwellautomation.com

About Aalo Atomics

Aalo Atomics is developing next-generation small modular reactor technologies designed to enable safe, scalable and cost-effective nuclear energy. Through participation in the U.S. Department of Energy Reactor Pilot Program, Aalo advances rapid reactor development and testing to support the future of clean energy.

AI Analysis

This announcement represents a significant convergence of industrial automation and next-generation nuclear energy. Rockwell's involvement validates the critical importance of proven control systems in advanced reactor deployment, particularly for companies pursuing rapid commercialization pathways. The timing is noteworthy—Aalo's achievement of criticality ahead of schedule demonstrates that modular reactor technology is advancing faster than traditional nuclear timelines, with automation playing a key role in accelerating deployment. For the broader energy transition, this partnership signals industry confidence in small modular reactors as viable solutions for powering data centers and other high-demand applications.

Blue Energy announces-PR Newswire

Source: https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/blue-energy-receives-strategic-investment-from-constellation-to-accelerate-commercialization-of-novel-shipyard-manufacturing-and-project-financing-model-for-new-nuclear-302827725.html

Blue Energy Receives Strategic Investment from Constellation to Accelerate Commercialization of Novel Shipyard Manufacturing and Project Financing Model for New Nuclear

July 16, 2026

TL;DR

  • Blue Energy announced a strategic equity investment from Constellation Technology Ventures, the venture arm of Constellation Energy (Nasdaq: CEG), marking the first investment by the venture arm in a U.S. nuclear developer advancing small modular reactors.
  • The investment supports Blue Energy's deployment strategy using shipyard manufacturing, proven reactor technology (GE Vernova's BWRX-300), and an innovative project-financeable model designed to make new nuclear development predictable, faster, and more affordable.
  • Blue Energy could begin early site works on its first planned project in Texas in 2026, with a final investment decision targeted for 2027 and power delivery goals of 48 months or less through its phased gas-to-nuclear deployment strategy.

Full Article

Blue Energy, a developer of financeable, prefabricated nuclear power plants, today announced a strategic equity investment from Constellation Technology Ventures, the venture arm of Constellation (Nasdaq: CEG), the nation's largest producer of clean energy and operator of the largest fleet of nuclear power plants in the United States. The investment reflects a growing confidence in Blue Energy's strategy to utilize shipyard manufacturing and project financing to deploy proven reactor technology that has the potential to accelerate new nuclear development – making it predictable, faster and more affordable. It also marks the first investment by Constellation Technology Ventures in a U.S. nuclear developer advancing small modular reactors.

"With demand for near-term power rising, Constellation's investment will help Blue Energy meet America's need by making new nuclear development predictable, rapidly scalable, and project financeable for the first time in history. This relationship helps us leverage an established operator, proven technology, and innovative, project-financeable deployment models to expand access to nuclear energy," said Jake Jurewicz, Blue Energy CEO and Co-Founder. "Together, we're demonstrating that the future of nuclear energy isn't a decade away and doesn't take a leap of faith on technology or construction execution, it's being built right now."
"Constellation is committed to exploring innovative pathways that can help accelerate the deployment of advanced nuclear technologies in the United States and allocate risk appropriately," said David Dardis, Constellation Senior Executive Vice President and Chief External Affairs and Growth Officer. "The Constellation Technology Ventures investment in Blue Energy supports its deployment plans for the GE Vernova Hitachi's BWRX-300, a proven technology with a potential path to scale for the next generation of nuclear energy."

Blue Energy's model is designed to address one of the biggest challenges facing the nuclear industry: how to finance and deploy new nuclear generation at the speed required to meet growing demand. By utilizing proven nuclear technology and employing an innovative large-format robotic prefabrication and assembly method inspired by offshore oil & gas and LNG projects, the company plans to unlock project financing for the first time in the nuclear sector and accelerate deployment timelines.

Earlier this year, Blue Energy announced it raised $380 million and forged a strategic partnership with GE Vernova to develop a multi-gigawatt gas-to-nuclear project utilizing GE Vernova gas turbines and BWRX-300 small modular reactors. The company also recently secured a key U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission licensing milestone that supports its goal of delivering reliable power in 48 months or less through its phased gas-to-nuclear deployment strategy. Blue Energy could begin early site works on its first planned project in Texas in 2026, to support a final investment decision in 2027.

About Blue Energy

Founded in 2023, Blue Energy develops financeable, turnkey nuclear power plants compatible with leading reactor technology. Our proprietary lower cost of capital solution and offsite pre-fabrication accelerates new nuclear deployment – making it predictable, faster and more affordable. We will deliver baseload power competitive with fossil fuels and renewables to meet unprecedented global demand. Blue Energy's world-class team has extensive experience in nuclear construction, licensing, engineering, and development. We stem from MIT's Nuclear Science & Engineering Department and are backed by VXI Capital, Engine Ventures, At One Ventures and Tamarack Global. Visit www.blueenergy.co or follow us on LinkedIn.

AI Analysis

This investment marks a significant milestone for small modular reactor (SMR) commercialization in the United States, demonstrating that major established nuclear operators are willing to back innovative deployment models. Blue Energy's approach of combining proven BWRX-300 technology with shipyard-style manufacturing and project financing represents a pragmatic solution to the nuclear industry's historical challenges with cost overruns and extended timelines. The aggressive 48-month deployment target for power generation, if achieved, would represent a substantial acceleration compared to traditional nuclear plants and could reshape investor confidence in the SMR sector.

Canary Media’s-Canary Media

Source: https://www.canarymedia.com/articles/nuclear/nuclear-energy-big-decade

Nuclear energy could be in for a big decade

Published: 17 July 2026

TL;DR

  • Global nuclear capacity is forecast to rise 44% from 2024 to 2036 (372 GW to 535 GW), driven by climate goals, energy security concerns, and rising electricity demand
  • 76 reactors are currently under construction worldwide, with China leading the buildout and potentially surpassing the U.S. as the global nuclear leader by 2030
  • The industry faces critical challenges around cost overruns and missed deadlines that must be overcome for this expansion to succeed

Overview

The global fleet of nuclear power plants is poised to expand quickly as climate goals, security concerns, and rising demand renew interest in the energy source.

By Dan McCarthy

Market Shift

The global nuclear energy industry has been muted since 2011, when the Fukushima Daiichi disaster unfolded in Japan. Some new reactors have been built — especially in China — but a lot of old nuclear power plants have been retired as well.

That means nuclear energy has been "running in place" for well over a decade, as a new report from BloombergNEF puts it. But now, the market is changing — and BNEF's analysts predict that the sector is poised to break out in a sprint.

Capacity Forecasts

BNEF forecasts that global nuclear energy capacity will rise to 535 gigawatts by 2036, a 44% increase from last year's installed capacity of 372 GW.

A simple reason for the optimistic forecast is that a lot of reactors are under construction worldwide: 76, according to BNEF, as of the first half of this year. Almost every single one is a conventional large-scale reactor, as opposed to a buzzy new small nuclear reactor. Those units represent 83 GW of new capacity on their own.

Drivers of Growth

Nuclear energy's turnaround comes as countries look to decarbonize their power grids, as it's a readily available source of carbon-free electricity. It's also just a big source of electricity, period — and global demand for power is rising fast. Several countries, including Japan, have also shown renewed interest in nuclear energy in light of the Iran war, which has roiled global oil and gas markets.

Global Leadership and Regional Developments

China is leading this new nuclear buildout. It's home to nearly half of all reactors under construction worldwide, and by 2030 it may have more nuclear capacity online than the U.S., the current global leader.

Officials in the U.S., for their part, are talking a big game about reinvigorating the domestic nuclear industry. Construction in the U.S. remains scant compared with China's dozens of in-progress reactors, but the sector is making some headway in the U.S. Two smaller, advanced reactors are underway in the U.S. as of this spring, and an 800-megawatt reactor in Michigan may come back online this year after missing a 2025 target.

Critical Challenges

The things to track now are timing and cost. In certain markets, especially the U.S., nuclear projects have been dogged by extreme cost overruns and blown deadlines — and that combination has held back the sector's growth. The new wave of interest gives the industry a chance to prove it can finish projects on budget and on time. We'll see if it delivers.

---

About the Author: Dan McCarthy is a senior editor at Canary Media.

AI Analysis

This article captures a pivotal moment in nuclear energy's evolution, highlighting the sector's potential to play a critical role in global decarbonization efforts. The optimistic forecast of 44% capacity growth by 2036 reflects growing recognition that nuclear power is essential for meeting both climate commitments and surging electricity demand, particularly as electrification accelerates. However, the author appropriately emphasizes that success hinges on the industry's ability to overcome historical challenges with cost control and project timelines—a realistic note that grounds the optimism in execution realities. The geopolitical dimensions, including Chinese leadership and renewed interest sparked by energy security concerns, underscore that nuclear expansion is driven by multiple converging factors beyond just climate policy.

KAERI announced-Korea Times

Source: https://www.koreatimes.co.kr/business/tech-science/20260716/koreas-molten-salt-nuclear-powered-container-ship-design-wins-us-approval

Korea's molten salt nuclear-powered container ship design wins US approval

Published Jul 16, 2026 4:36 pm KST

TL;DR

  • South Korea's Korea Atomic Energy Research Institute (KAERI) achieved Approval in Principle (AIP) from the American Bureau of Shipping for a 15,000-TEU container ship powered by a molten salt reactor (MSR).
  • The ship design features two MSR units with optimized power systems, dual-reactor load-sharing, energy storage capabilities, and a 25-knot hull design that fits through the expanded Panama Canal.
  • This represents a major milestone for nuclear-powered commercial vessels, with KAERI, KRISO, and Samsung Heavy Industries collaborating on the technology developed under the MARINA program.

Full Article

By Jhoo Dong-chan

The Korea Atomic Energy Research Institute (KAERI) said Thursday that a conceptual design for a 15,000-TEU container ship powered by a molten salt reactor (MSR) has received Approval in Principle (AIP) from the American Bureau of Shipping, a U.S.-based ship classification society.

The design was jointly developed with the Korea Research Institute of Ships & Ocean Engineering (KRISO) and Samsung Heavy Industries.

AIP is a process in which a classification society reviews the technical feasibility and safety of a new technology or design at the concept stage, based on relevant regulations and safety standards — a significant step for a field still in its early stages of international development, such as nuclear-powered ships.

An MSR is a type of small modular reactor that uses molten salt mixed with nuclear fuel as a liquid fuel, prized for its high safety and energy efficiency as a next-generation ship engine.

The container ship design uses two MSR units, with optimized power systems and hull layout meant to improve both safety and operational efficiency.

Key features include a dual-reactor setup for load-sharing, an energy storage system for managing surplus power, a high-speed hull design capable of 25 knots, a centrally located reactor to minimize wave and collision risks, and a hull sized to pass through the expanded Panama Canal.

KAERI developed the marine MSR technology, called MARINA, while KRISO and Samsung Heavy Industries handled the hull design, reactor and system layout and power control technology.

The institutions plan to further refine the reactor-ship interface and pursue safety verification and testing.

AI Analysis

This achievement represents a significant milestone in maritime decarbonization and advanced nuclear technology deployment. The molten salt reactor technology offers compelling advantages for maritime applications—superior safety characteristics, efficient fuel utilization, and zero-emission operations—making it an attractive solution for the shipping industry's stringent environmental regulations. The collaborative effort between KAERI, KRISO, and Samsung Heavy Industries demonstrates how integrating reactor development with shipbuilding expertise accelerates practical commercialization timelines. The AIP approval validates the technical feasibility of the concept and positions South Korea as a leader in next-generation nuclear propulsion systems for commercial vessels.

MIT professor develops method to verify compliance with Outer Space Treaty-Nuclear Newswire

Source: https://www.ans.org/news/2026-07-16/article-8211/mit-professor-develops-method-to-verify-compliance-with-outer-space-treaty/

MIT Professor Develops Method to Verify Compliance with Outer Space Treaty

July 16, 2026

TL;DR

  • MIT professor Areg Danagoulian has developed a spallation-based satellite detection system to verify compliance with the 1967 Outer Space Treaty, which prohibits nuclear weapons in space
  • The proposed inspector satellite would use high-energy proton emissions to detect uranium or plutonium in nearby satellites with 99% accuracy within one week at 4,000-meter distance
  • This physics-based verification method could provide more reliable nonproliferation assurance than conventional intelligence-gathering, addressing long-standing gaps in treaty enforcement mechanisms

Article

Areg Danagoulian of the Department of Nuclear Science and Engineering at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology is proposing a mechanism for verifying that Earth-orbiting satellites are in compliance with the Outer Space Treaty, which prohibits the placement of nuclear weapons in space. Danagoulian's "concept and feasibility study," titled "Verification of the Outer Space Treaty with cosmic protons," was published recently in the journal Nature.

No Verification and No Trust

Since 1967, a total of 117 countries have signed the Outer Space Treaty, including the three countries with the most nuclear weapons: Russia, the United States, and China. However, as Danagoulian states in his paper, "No methodologies of verification have been proposed in the open peer-reviewed literature."

Moreover, U.S. officials have alleged that Russia has a program to develop a nuclear anti-satellite weapon for deployment in Earth orbit. In the current conflict-plagued global geopolitical environment, other nations likely hold similar concerns. If nations had a way of verifying other nations' compliance with the Outer Space Treaty, it would help allay such worries.

Spallation

Danagoulian developed a concept for a satellite-based sensor system that would use spallation (the release of particles due to high-energy collisions) to detect if a nearby satellite is carrying a nuclear weapon. The detector, or inspector, satellite would emit highly energetic protons that, if colliding with a radioactive element like uranium or plutonium in a nuclear weapon, would cause the release of large numbers of neutrons into space. Scintillators on the inspector satellite would be able to detect the released neutrons, which would serve as evidence of a nuclear weapon.

According to Danagoulian's calculations, an inspector satellite equipped with such a spallation system would be able to accurately detect the presence of a nuclear weapon within one hour by flying by a suspect satellite within a distance of 1,000 meters. Given an observation distance of 4,000 meters, the inspector satellite would be able to identify a nuclear weapon with 99 percent accuracy within about a week. The use of multiple inspection satellites would reduce that time.

Danagoulian hopes his study "will stimulate and inform future research and development of verification platforms for the OST," he said in his paper. In an article in MIT News, he explained, "I say in the paper this isn't a completely proven system. The purpose of the paper is to show the scientific community that it's scientifically possible to do this. But there are many more practical considerations to be made to actually build these detectors."

Better Than Intelligence

You can fake intelligence, but you can't fake physics.

If such detectors are built, Danagoulian believes they will prove far more accurate than conventional intelligence-gathering methods that have been used in nonproliferation efforts.

He emphasized, "I very much hope this will turn into a real system, or proof-of-concept system, but the goal right now is to get national labs to use this work for their own research, and to get policymakers to seriously consider this technology as a potential part of national technical means."

AI Analysis

Danagoulian's spallation-based verification system represents a significant theoretical advancement in space arms control, shifting nonproliferation verification from subjective intelligence assessments to objective physics-based detection. The method's reliance on fundamental nuclear physics principles makes it theoretically more resistant to deception than conventional monitoring approaches. However, the transition from proof-of-concept to operational deployment faces substantial practical challenges, including international agreement on inspection protocols, satellite engineering constraints, and geopolitical acceptance of intrusive verification mechanisms. If realized, this technology could establish new precedent for technical verification of arms control treaties beyond space, potentially reshaping how major powers approach compliance assurance.

A closer look at NRC’s proposed rule eliminating ALARA-Nuclear Newswire

Source: https://www.ans.org/news/2026-07-13/article-8199/a-closer-look-at-nrcs-proposed-rule-eliminating-alara/

A closer look at NRC's proposed rule eliminating ALARA

July 13, 2026

TL;DR

  • The NRC proposed eliminating the "as low as reasonably achievable" (ALARA) principle from radiation protection regulations, replacing it with a graded approach to dose management.
  • The proposal aims to reduce regulatory ambiguity while maintaining existing dose limits unchanged, following executive order 14300's directive to adopt science-based radiation limits.
  • Current licensees would remain compliant without changes to existing programs, though the shift away from ALARA represents a significant departure from over 50 years of U.S. radiation safety practice.
Research & Applications

A closer look at NRC's proposed rule eliminating ALARA

July 13, 2026, 5:01PMNuclear News

On July 1, the Nuclear Regulatory Commission proposed removing the "as low as reasonably achievable" (ALARA) principle from its radiation protection regulations and replacing it with a graded approach. The agency also proposed increases to effluent dose limits and adjusting how it handles allowances for exceeding dose limits.

According to the proposed rule, the NRC expects that by complying with the existing rules, current licensees will already be compliant with the updated rule. They would have the option to—but not be required to—change their existing programs.

"We're raising the standard for regulatory clarity, not lowering the standard for safety," NRC Chairman Ho Nieh said in a press release. "Our radiation dose limits remain unchanged—what we're eliminating is unnecessary ambiguity."

Executive pressure: In May 2025, executive order (EO) 14300 called for a slew of changes at the NRC. Among these was an order to "adopt science-based radiation limits," specifically, to reconsider ALARA and the linear no-threshold (LNT) model for radiation exposure on which it's based, which the EO describes as "flawed."

In response, the NRC's proposal reexamines 10 CFR Part 20, which sets radiation protection standards for all NRC licensees and Agreement States. The NRC's proposal was released nearly one year after Idaho National Laboratory issued a report which made a case for eliminating ALARA and went a step further by also recommending higher occupational and public dose limits.

The NRC states that its revisions aim to "address unnecessary conservatism and excessive subjectivity" associated with regulating low doses of radiation and "incorporate flexibility and acceptable alternatives."

The rule change also discusses the limits of the LNT model but finds that "no consensus-supported, regulation-ready alternative model exists at this time."

The proposal's only mention of hormesis by name does not explicitly state the agency's current position on the theory that low doses of radiation can have potential beneficial effects, but it quotes the agency's 2021 position, stating that there is no compelling evidence for radiation hormesis. However, in the section describing effluents, the release of radioactive material into the environment, the NRC states without citing a source that "there is evidence for adaptive cellular response, which would mitigate the health effects of exposures at low doses, especially those resulting from effluents."

Amir Bahadori, a professor at Kansas State University and certified health physicist, said he thinks the NRC's response to the EO is "generally consistent with current scientific knowledge, the collective experience in the field of radiation protection, and the NRC's regulatory experience," but notes that the agency doesn't acknowledge key studies that support the continued use of the LNT model.

Low-dose radiation science: While the health impacts of higher-dose radiation are clear, it has proven difficult to measure the effect of radiation at low doses.

Many factors impact cancer risk, including age, weight, diet, cigarette smoking, alcohol consumption, physical inactivity, and asbestos exposure, and there is a high baseline rate of cancer occurrence. As a result, small population low-dose radiation studies have lacked sufficient statistical power to draw strong conclusions, and current dosimetric models are partially reliant on extrapolation from high-dose data.

The International Nuclear Workers Study (INWORKS) and the Million Person Study (MPS) aim to address this gap by studying large groups of radiation workers. Both studies are ongoing, though so far published results support a linear association between some types of cancer risk and cumulative dose, even at low dose levels.

So far, there is no established non-zero threshold dose level below which risk is known to become zero, and this has driven the interpretation of ALARA that the NRC is aiming to move away from.

ALARA in practice: The NRC has proposed removing references to the ALARA principle from its regulations, moving instead to a graded approach for managing radiation doses below regulatory limits.

It would be a significant shift—ALARA has been a cornerstone of U.S. radiation safety for more than 50 years.

In the proposed rule change, the NRC said the issue at the regulatory level is more about implementation than the standards themselves. The agency argues that ALARA has been interpreted as requiring dose minimization "without a reasonable stopping point," which has led to an expectation that dose reduction methods be applied whenever available, regardless of the amount of reduction offered, compared with total dose.

Bahadori said he approves of ALARA at its core but agrees it has been "consistently misinterpreted."

Nicole Martinez, a professor at Clemson University and certified health physicist, acknowledged that there is a tension between wanting to enable workers to use their professional judgment and needing regulatory mechanisms that support workers in getting the protection they deserve.

As a member of the International Commission on Radiological Protection (ICRP), she said she tends to think about ALARA as the ICRP does, applying the principle as a consideration for optimization of dose limits decisions rather than at the application step.

"I see ALARA as that sweet spot that's supposed to be able to account for prevailing circumstances," she said.

In the proposed rule, the NRC said that retiring the ALARA terminology supports a return to its original intent, "utilizing selected aspects of the linear dose response model in favor of a strict adherence to the LNT model."

Replacing ALARA: The NRC has proposed moving to a graded approach to dose management, establishing radiation protection actions that are triggered at particular levels of prospective or actual exposure to provide "reasonable assurance" that regulatory dose limits aren't exceeded.

The agency describes a "go/no-go" regulatory approach that "would deemphasize radiation protection precautions in lieu of verifications that a licensee is maintaining dose values below the applicable dose limit."

Despite her appreciation of ALARA, Martinez said she's in favor of changing the regulatory language to a graded approach.

"I like reasonable assurance of adequate protection," she said. "But I also understand that just saying something is adequate doesn't necessarily make it adequate."

The NRC already has an established annual dose limit, requiring that radiation exposure is monitored and that a radiation worker's total effective dose equivalent for the whole body does not exceed 5 rem in a year.

The current rule requires an exemption request to exceed this limit, such as during maintenance periods when exposure may be higher. One of the proposed changes adds some flexibility to this policy, allowing doses of up to twice the annual limit as long as a five-year average remains below the annual limit.

In the rule change proposal, the agency said it is still developing an explanation of what will be considered an acceptable approach but gave as an example "expected doses of 100 mrem/year, 500 mrem/year, and 5 rem/year," as a set of thresholds where corresponding actions such as radiation training, individual dose monitoring, and use of a dose limit extension, respectively, may be required.

Martinez said she thinks 500 mrem/year as the cutoff for receiving an individual dosimeter is too high and that 100 mrem/year would be more appropriate. She also noted that the NRC does not establish a lifetime limit, claiming it's not practical to track.

"One hundred millisieverts [10 rem] [lifetime risk] is cited up, down, left, right as where our uncertainties really get high in the dose response models, but there are studies that show that there is attributable risk lower than that," she said. "The extension of the dose limits over 50 mSv in a year doesn't necessarily concern me on its own, but . . . I don't see how you can have an extension of the annual dose limit without considering lifetime exposure and be consistent with what we know about radiogenic risk."

Under the proposed rule change, licensees would be permitted to conduct cost-benefit analyses to determine if they should implement a radiation protection measure or allow the dose to accrue, as long the choice complies with applicable dose limits.

More than radiation workers: ALARA currently extends across all radiation management contexts, and the NRC has also proposed a graded approach to public dose limits and effluents.

The NRC's public dose limit is 100 mrem/year, 2 percent of the limit for radiation workers. It applies in contexts such as medical waiting rooms, where members of the public may be exposed to low levels of radiation. Under the graded approach, licensees would not be required to analyze projected public doses less than 25 mrem/year, and they would be permitted to conduct cost-benefit analyses when considering radiation management options for doses greater than 25 mrem/year.

For effluents, the NRC has proposed increasing its limit from 10 mrem/year to 25 mrem/year, which it said changes excess fatal cancer risk over 70 years from 0.04 cases per 100 people to 0.09 cases per 100 people.

Regardless of the effluent level measured, licensees will only be required to collect and retain effluent data on an annual basis. If a licensee measures above 25 mrem/year, it would be required to "evaluate and consider implementing cost-justified corrective actions to restore effluent levels to below the 25 mrem/year constraint."

If the licensee's cost analysis determines that corrective actions are not justified, the licensee is given the option to propose a new constraint for itself above 25 mrem/year, as long as it's lower than 100 mrem/year. New facilities would also be allowed to propose an effluent constraint level in that range with cost justification.

"I am supportive of establishing [a public dose optimization] threshold, but I think the appropriate dose value should be determined only after extensive stakeholder engagement," Bahadori said. "The very low effluent ALARA objectives and emissions limits currently in the regulations make it very straightforward to rebut claims that nuclear power plants cause measurable adverse health effects for the general public. Even small increased doses to the public make it more difficult to defend against such claims."

Justifications: Part of the NRC's justification for the changes is a cost-benefit analysis it conducted that found the changes would save the nuclear industry, Agreement States, and the NRC $9.53 million, $244,000, and $704,000 per year, respectively.

Bahadori is said these savings are "quite small" relative to annual operating costs and could easily become a wash.

"If doses to radiation workers increase even slightly, or if the workers' perception of their risk changes, workers might demand greater compensation, potentially offsetting these cost savings," he said.

Does this achieve the stated goals? The NRC cites addressing aspects of the low-dose radiation standards that are susceptible to inconsistent reinforcement or unnecessary conservatism as a key goal.

"I think the NRC did a good job of responding to the president's executive order," said Bahadori. "There are, however, some fairly definitive statements in the proposed rule regarding conservatism built into the LNT model that do not fully align with current ICRP and NCRP [National Council on Radiation Protection and Measurements] recommendations."

Martinez said her primary worry is unintended consequences that the language in the proposed rule change may not cover, such as employers offering rewards for workers receiving a higher dose, which muddies informed and voluntary consent.

"I worry about workers maybe having access to less information, maybe having less autonomy when it comes to exposures," said Martinez. "The rule also argues that there's a problem of subjectivity. I don't necessarily disagree with that, but I'm not convinced that some of the proposed changes aren't just a different type of subjectivity."

Recent history: ALARA was introduced in 1971, and the last major revision to 10 CFR Part 20 was in 1991, where the most significant change was adopting the concept of "effective dose," which provides a combined assessment of internal and external radiation exposures and relies on the LNT model.

In 2015, the NRC received petitions for rulemaking to discontinue use of the LNT model as the primary basis for its standards, including removing ALARA. Some of the petitions' arguments overlapped with those in EO 14300, claiming that the NRC's implementation of ALARA has led to excessive costs.

The NRC denied the petitions, arguing that "compliance with the ALARA requirement is based on whether the licensee has incorporated reasonable measures to track and, if necessary, to reduce exposures—not whether exposures and doses represent an absolute minimum or whether the licensee has used all possible methods to reduce exposures."

According to the new proposed rule change, the agency said it maintains that position while also saying that its lack of clarity, excessive subjectivity, and inconsistent enforcement of ALARA justifies removing use of the principle.

In a recent article, George Joslin, Arden Rowell, Seyed Reihani, and Zahra Mohaghegh outline the shifting legal framework around nuclear regulation since the passing of the 2024 Advanced Nuclear for Clean Energy (ADVANCE) Act and how it is driving changes to how radiation dose is considered.

According to the article, what has changed "is not the existence of uncertainty in radiation science but the institutional requirement that such uncertainty be addressed explicitly within a legal framework that demands justification rather than reliance on judicial deference."

Voice your opinion: The NRC will accept public comments on the proposed changes for 45 days following publication in the _Federal Register_ , which has yet to occur. It will hold a public meeting during the comment period.

AI Analysis

The NRC's proposed rule eliminating the ALARA principle represents a significant regulatory shift aimed at reducing what the agency characterizes as unnecessary conservatism in radiation protection standards, though it remains grounded in unchanged dose limits. This change responds to executive pressure to adopt "science-based" radiation limits but occurs amid ongoing scientific debate, as recent large-scale studies of radiation workers continue to support linear dose-response relationships even at low levels. The proposal's introduction of a graded approach offers regulatory flexibility to licensees while maintaining that current compliance programs already meet the new standards, potentially streamlining operations without reducing safety margins in practice. However, the rule change highlights unresolved tensions between precautionary principles and emerging scientific perspectives on low-dose radiation effects, particularly regarding adaptive cellular responses versus established linear no-threshold models. The outcome of this proposed rule will likely influence global radiation protection policy and how different regulatory frameworks balance scientific evolution with public health precaution.

English

A closer look at the NRC’s reactor licensing revamp-Nuclear Newswire

Source: https://www.ans.org/news/2026-07-10/article-8195/a-closer-look-at-the-nrcs-reactor-licensing-revamp/

A closer look at the NRC's reactor licensing revamp

Published: July 10, 2026

TL;DR

  • The NRC proposed a comprehensive modernization of reactor licensing rules that could save $1.86 billion and accelerate deployment of new reactor technologies
  • Key changes include expedited construction pathways, extended manufacturing and operating licenses, and more flexible siting criteria for diverse reactor types
  • The rule replaces rigid frameworks with risk-informed, performance-based alternatives to reduce exemptions and regulatory burden while maintaining safety

Overview

Make no mistake about it: the Nuclear Regulatory Commission's proposed rulemaking on reactor licensing, safety oversight, and siting practices is expansive. When it was unveiled on July 1, NRC officials called it the "most comprehensive modernization of reactor licensing in decades," one that could save the NRC and the industry as much as $1.86 billion.

"NRC's regulations have not kept pace with new technologies and our energy needs," NRC Chairman Ho Nieh said in an announcement. "This proposed rule strips out rigid frameworks and unnecessary conservatism to accelerate the safe deployment of new reactors and expand existing capacity across America."
The proposed rule, "Modernizing Reactor Licensing, Safety Oversight, and Siting Practices," spans just over 550 pages. At first glance one may understandably not know where to start. Fortunately, _Nuclear News_ took a deeper dive into the massive rulemaking, focusing on 17 key provisions that the NRC identified in the rule.

At this writing, the proposed rule has not yet been published in the _Federal Register_. Once published, it will be open for public comment for 45 days. Below are summaries on these key changes.

Expedited Construction

Some potential nuclear reactor developers would like to construct non-safety-significant portions of a nuclear facility ahead of a construction permit or combined license, the NRC says. Under the proposed rule, the agency would add language to create a general license to begin this type of construction upon docketing an application. Furthermore, the NRC would update its definition of "construction" to include structures, systems, and components (SSCs) for which "construction can affect the SSC's capability to perform a safety-related or safety-significant function and will, therefore, require NRC approval before commencing construction." The argument here is that defining which SSCs fall into which category will help facilitate nuclear power plant construction.

Reactor Design Changes

Executive Order 14300 directed the NRC to establish stringent thresholds for reactor design changes during construction and operation. In response, the agency proposes amending its regulations regarding reactor design changes during construction and operation. The changes under 10 CFR Parts 52 and 53 would establish "appropriate thresholds for NRC-initiated changes as well as provide additional flexibility and reduce unnecessary regulatory burden for licensee-initiated changes." Of note is the proposal to eliminate "increased standardization" as a criterion for changes in design certification.

Revising Emergency Preparedness

Changes here would amend regulations to create more adaptable pathways for emergency preparedness. The NRC argues that this will create a "strengthened, more risk-informed approach" to emergency preparedness and will provide greater assurances in the event of a radiological emergency.

Operational Programs

Developers applying for a manufacturing license could voluntarily submit operational programs for NRC review and approval as part of the license review under this provision. The NRC notes here the interest for "rapid, widespread" deployment of new reactors of a standard design, supported by NRC staff recommendations.

"These agency actions would support the rapid licensing and high-volume deployment of new microreactors and other low consequence reactors and the direction in EO 14300 to adopt shorter timeframes tailored to particular licensing pathways," according to the rule.

Early Site Permits

Amendments to early site permit licensing and approval processes would eliminate the requirement for a permit expiration date, clarify the applicability of permit requirements, and propose conforming amendments to enhance the agency's effectiveness and efficiency in licensing and approvals.

Reactor Manufacturing License Extensions

The proposed rule would extend a manufacturing license's lifespan to 40 years and extend renewals to 40 years, as well. Currently, a manufacturing license lasts a minimum of 5 years and a maximum of 15 years.

"The current landscape for nuclear power reactors designs, which encompasses potential microreactors and small modular reactors in addition to traditional large LWRs, may significantly benefit from the regulatory stability afforded by a longer ML term," the rule said.

40-Year Operating License Renewals

The length of operating license renewals would increase from 20 years to 40 years under the proposed rulemaking.

"Most U.S. reactors are already operating beyond 40 years under renewed licenses and are following their approved aging management programs," the rule says.

Reactor Site Criteria Flexibility

All stationary power reactor applications submitted after January 10, 1997, follow siting criteria in subpart B of 10 CFR Part 100. Current criteria are more prescriptive and tend to cater to large sites for LWRs, according to the rule. The proposed criteria changes would be more flexible to better accommodate a broader range of reactor technologies. The NRC would still maintain its preference for siting reactors in less-populated areas. It would, however, provide flexibility for siting reactors in areas of greater population density as long as it is "justified by an assessment comparing the societal risks and societal benefits of siting reactors in those areas."

Decommissioning Funding Assurances

This provision would allow certain new reactor applicants and licensees to submit a design-specific decommissioning cost estimate that is less than the approved table of values found in 10 CFR 50.75(c). It is something the NRC is also considering for 10 CFR Part 53, intended to more broadly accommodate new reactor technologies.

More Alternatives

The NRC would allow licensees to request a broader scope of alternatives to the requirements in 10 CFR 50.55a(z), "Codes and standards." The argument here is that the expansion would allow for added flexibility without requiring exemptions; reducing or eliminating exemptions is a recurring theme in the rulemaking.

"This proposed action would allow nuclear power plant licensees and applicants for construction permits (CP), operating licenses (OL), combined licenses (COL), standard design certifications, standard design approvals, and manufacturing licenses (ML) to request authorization of voluntary alternatives to a broader scope of requirements in 10 CFR 50.55a," according to the rule.

Increasing Flexibility

Two changes would be made to 10 CFR 50.59, "Changes, tests, and experiments." These changes would allow the use of quantitative risk results to demonstrate that a change to a facility does not result in a "more than minimal increase," as well as allow licensees to make changes to methods that previously required NRC review. This second item would only be allowed if the licensee adopted an acceptable verification, validation, and uncertainty qualification program.

"This shift would enable licensee-led evaluations and allow for the evaluation of advanced modeling methods through structured processes rather than fixed requirements," according to the rule.

Streamlining QA Criteria

This proposed appendix to 10 CFR Part 50, "Streamlined Quality assurance criteria for nuclear power plants and fuel reprocessing plants," would provide an alternative to a separate appendix that has been a mainstay for establishing minimum quality assurance requirements for safety-related SSCs. Concerns have been raised about how the older appendix fits into modern practices, according to the rule. The new appendix would establish performance-based QA criteria that would provide direction on using a graded approach for QA requirements regarding SSCs, would incorporate QA terminology and methodologies used across industries, would provide enhanced regulatory certainty, and would remove NRC oversight of suppliers and vendors of products and services, when appropriate.

Language Updates

Updates to language abound in the proposed rule. One noted by the NRC regards proposed changes to language surrounding construction permit requirements and related licenses. According to the rule, these updates would more clearly link the level of detail required to be submitted with a CP application to the findings the NRC requires in 10 CFR 50.35(a), 50.40, and 50.50 to issue the permit. This would also adjust some of the light water reactor–centric language to be more technology-inclusive.

Alternative Risk-Informed, Performance-Based Criteria

The proposed rule would add new, standalone provisions providing licensees and applicants technology-inclusive, risk-informed, or performance-based criteria as alternatives to existing requirements that are more prescriptive.

"These new provisions would support the expanded and accelerated use of acceptance criteria reflective of innovative nuclear technologies without the need for exemptions, while continuing to ensure reasonable assurance of adequate protection of public health and safety," the rule states.

This would also include updating and clarifying its general design criteria regulations, allowing for deviations from these regulations without requiring exemptions.

Increased Enrichment for Fuel Designs

The NRC wants to improve its licensing of fuels enriched above 5.0 weight percent uranium-235 by updating its regulations. The agency argues that the proposed updates would reduce the number of exemption requests from existing regulations.

"The new requirements also would address fuel fragmentation, relocation, and dispersal in relation to the key accident tolerant fuel components of increased enrichment and burnup limits," the rule says.

Design-Basis and Beyond-Design-Basis Events

This provision would add the terms "design-basis event" and "beyond-design-basis event" to the definitions section of 10 CFR 50.2, as well as a conforming revision to the definition of "design-basis event" in 10 CFR 50.49, "Environmental qualification of electric equipment important to safety for nuclear power plants."

Footnote Removal

Under this provision, the NRC would delete a footnote that clarifies safety-related electric equipment as Class 1E equipment under Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers Standard 323-1974. The NRC considers the footnote unnecessary at this point, having established the connection between safety-related electric equipment and Class 1E equipment.

AI Analysis

This modernization represents a significant shift toward technology-agnostic, risk-informed regulation that prioritizes flexibility and efficiency without sacrificing safety oversight. The NRC's comprehensive approach addresses the regulatory barriers that have historically hindered rapid deployment of advanced reactor designs, including small modular reactors and microreactors. By extending license durations, allowing performance-based alternatives, and reducing reliance on exemptions, the rulemaking could accelerate the commercialization of diverse nuclear technologies while maintaining rigorous safety standards through performance-based criteria rather than prescriptive requirements.

House subcommittee OKs six nuclear permitting reform bills-Nuclear Newswire

Source: https://www.ans.org/news/2026-07-15/article-8206/house-subcommittee-oks-six-nuclear-permitting-reform-bills/

House Subcommittee OKs Six Nuclear Permitting Reform Bills

Published: July 15, 2026

TL;DR

  • The House Energy and Commerce Committee's Subcommittee on Energy advanced all six nuclear permitting reform bills with bipartisan support, including measures to streamline licensing for fuel recycling and uranium enrichment facilities.
  • The bills propose significant changes including making uncontested NRC hearings optional, reforming the Advisory Committee on Reactor Safeguards (ACRS) role, increasing NRC staff compensation to compete with private industry, and mandating greater Department of Energy transparency.
  • Key concerns remain about the ACRS reform bill potentially restricting independent oversight capabilities, with some lawmakers advocating to preserve the committee's ability to identify issues the NRC may have missed.

Overview

The U.S. House Energy and Commerce Committee's Subcommittee on Energy introduced six nuclear permitting reform bills back in June. The mostly bipartisan bills returned to the subcommittee on Tuesday for markups—and with strong backing from both sides of the aisle.

Despite some lingering concerns in areas like federal transparency, the role of the Advisory Committee on Reactor Safeguards (ACRS), and staff attrition in the Nuclear Regulatory Commission, the subcommittee advanced all six pieces of legislation to the full committee with bipartisan support.

"We can't let the licensing process itself be a major impediment to the maintaining of our edge" in global nuclear energy, said Rep. Brett Guthrie (R., Ky.).

The Six Bills

Nuclear Recycling Efficient Fuels Utilizing Expedited Licensing (REFUEL) Act

The proposed REFUEL Act amends the Atomic Energy Act and clarifies that certain nuclear fuel recycling facilities can be licensed under regulatory frameworks used in other types of fuel facilities, instead of as production facilities. Lawmakers argue this would expedite the licensing process for these facilities. The subcommittee advanced this bill as well as an amendment that clarifies that the bill would apply to recycling facilities that do not isolate plutonium, and that the proposed changes do not alter existing export requirements.

American Enrichment Deployment Act

The subcommittee also moved ahead with the American Enrichment Deployment Act, which was introduced as a draft last month. This legislation further amends the Atomic Energy Act by updating the licensing procedures for uranium enrichment facilities. The intent here is to align the licensing of enrichment facilities with other fuel cycle facilities and allow the construction of these facilities to begin prior to licensing. Bill sponsors Rep. Russell Fry (R., S.C.) and Kim Schrier (D., Wash.) said streamlining this licensing process is needed as the U.S. seeks to expand domestic enrichment and move away from Russian uranium imports.

Efficient Nuclear Licensing Hearings Act

Lawmakers also advanced the Efficient Nuclear Licensing Hearings Act, which would make uncontested hearings the NRC conducts in its licensing reviews optional instead of mandatory. Current law requires applications for construction permits, early site permits, and combined licenses to include a hearing even if no party requested one.

Nuclear Advisory Committee Reform Act

Perhaps the most contested bill of the six is the Nuclear Advisory Committee Reform Act, which would update the role of the NRC's ACRS in licensing and oversight and codify the directive in Executive Order 14300 for the independent technical group to function at a statutory minimum and that its permitting and licensing reviews "shall focus on issues that are truly novel and noteworthy."

While the bill did get the OK from the subcommittee, some members said the bill as written would restrict the ACRS's role and further limit its ability to independently check reviews and identify any issues the NRC may have missed.

"I don't think that we should be shifting to a model where the ACRS only has an ability to say something if the NRC asks them to," said Rep. Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez (D., N.Y.).

Bill sponsor Rep. Diana Harshbarger (R., Tenn.), said she would work together with subcommittee members who have expressed concerns on potential changes agreed on by both sides ahead of the bill moving to the full committee.

NRC Staff Pay Alignment Act

Next up was the proposed NRC Staff Pay Alignment Act, which would seek to better compensate NRC staff and give the federal agency a better chance to compete against the private nuclear industry sector for workers, such as by increasing pay for senior executive service positions.

Department of Energy Nuclear Transparency Act

Finally, the subcommittee advanced the Department of Energy Nuclear Transparency Act, which would require greater transparency from the DOE in its authorization of nuclear reactors.

"If we want modern nuclear energy to succeed in the United States, we must maintain the public trust in the industry. Congress and the American people should have full transparency into what the Department of Energy is doing," said bill sponsor Rep. Kathy Castor (D., Fla.).

The subcommittee added an amendment to the Department of Energy Nuclear Transparency Act that would add and protect classified and security-sensitive information from disclosure under Freedom of Information Act exemptions, as well as adjust public reporting timelines.

AI Analysis

This legislative package represents a significant modernization effort for nuclear permitting in the United States, addressing both procedural streamlining and workforce retention challenges. The bipartisan support demonstrates broad recognition that the current licensing framework may hinder domestic nuclear expansion at a critical moment for energy policy. However, the debate over ACRS authority highlights an ongoing tension between expediting approvals and maintaining independent technical oversight—a balance essential for preserving public confidence in nuclear safety decisions. These bills, if enacted, could meaningfully reduce the time and cost burden for new nuclear facilities while the transparency amendments ensure congressional and public accountability.

The NRC has-World Nuclear News

Source: https://www.world-nuclear-news.org/articles/nrc-exempts-westinghouse-from-design-certification-renewal-rule

NRC exempts Westinghouse from design certification renewal rule

Friday, 10 July 2026

TL;DR

  • The US Nuclear Regulatory Commission granted Westinghouse an exemption to apply for early renewal of the AP1000 design certification, ahead of the standard 2043-2045 renewal window
  • The exemption allows Westinghouse to incorporate lessons learned from the construction and operation of Vogtle units 3 and 4, including design changes that departed from the original certified design
  • This strategic move supports President Trump's vision for a US fleet of large nuclear reactors and enables potential fleet-scale deployment of the AP1000 modular reactor

Full Article

_32779.jpg) (Image: Westinghouse)

The US Nuclear Regulatory Commission has said Westinghouse can apply to renew the AP1000 standard design certification, incorporating lessons learned from the construction and operation of Vogtle units 3 and 4, well ahead of the expiration of the current certificate.

The regulator first certified the AP1000 design in 2006. That certification is valid until 2046.

Under current rules, companies can only apply for the renewal of a design certificate "not less than 12 nor more than 36 months before the expiration of the initial 40-year period".

On 7 April, Westinghouse submitted a request to the Nuclear Regulatory Commission (NRC) seeking a 40-year renewal of the certification while incorporating design changes approved during the construction and licensing of Vogtle Units 3 and 4, many of which represented departures from the original certified design. The submittal of Revision 20 of the AP1000 Design Control Document (DCD) to the NRC is part of Westinghouse's strategic plan to enable a fleet-scale deployment of the advanced AP1000 modular reactor and support President Donald Trump's vision to build a US fleet of large nuclear reactors, the company said. In order to apply for the early renewal of the AP1000 design certification (DC), Westinghouse submitted a request for exemption from the scheduling requirements of 10 CFR 52.57(a).

On 27 April, the NRC staff determined the exemption request contained sufficient information for the NRC staff to initiate a detailed technical review and accepted the exemption request for docketing. This exemption request has been considered separately from the AP1000 design certification renewal request and associated amendment.

The NRC has now determined that, pursuant to relevant regulation, "the exemption is authorised by law, will not present an undue risk to the public health and safety, and is consistent with the common defence and security". It noted that delaying submission of the AP1000 design certification renewal application until 2043 could lead to inefficiencies for both the NRC staff and applicants in connection with future combined licence application submittals that reference the AP1000 design certification.

"Therefore, the Commission hereby grants Westinghouse a one-time exemption from the requirement in 10 CFR 52.57(a) that an application for renewal of a DC must be submitted not less than 12 nor more than 36 months before expiration of the initial 40-year period, such that Westinghouse may apply for renewal of the AP1000 DC more than 36 months before the expiration of the initial 40-year period," the NRC said in a Federal Registry entry.

In addition to the Vogtle units, Westinghouse says four AP1000 reactors are currently in operation in China, with 12 additional reactors under construction. The design has been localised in China as the CAP1000.

AI Analysis

This exemption represents a significant administrative milestone for Westinghouse's AP1000 reactor expansion strategy, removing a temporal barrier to certification renewal that would have otherwise delayed the process by approximately 17 years. By enabling early incorporation of operational lessons from Vogtle units 3 and 4, the NRC decision streamlines the certification process and reduces redundancy in future licensing reviews. The exemption aligns with broader US energy policy objectives and the international competitiveness of the AP1000 design, particularly given China's parallel development and deployment of derived CAP1000 models, making regulatory efficiency a strategic advantage in the global nuclear market.
